## 8,893

B"H, 30 Tishrei, 5725
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of Monday, in which you write regarding the young woman (may she live) who met a non-Jew, etc., and after much discussion and a significant amount of time, the non-Jew says that he wants to convert properly and is prepared to accept upon himself the yoke of the mitzvos, etc., etc. You are in doubt as to how to relate to this proposal, given that the entire matter began through the acquaintance with the aforementioned young woman, etc.

Generally, it is not my practice to issue halachic rulings, and there are many individuals qualified for this in a great city like New York. Nevertheless, since you have already written, and as a suggestion of logic rather than a halachic ruling, I will offer the following lines.

As is evident from your letter, the connection between the aforementioned parties is very strong, and who knows, etc. On the other hand, even regarding a conversion that has no doubt attached to it, the statement of our Sages regarding those who turn away, etc., is well known. In the situation described, there is certainly room for concern regarding the blemish to the family, etc.

Seemingly, the most straightforward approach in this matter is to suggest to the non-Jew—since, according to his words, his intention is for the sake of conversion—that he surely also admits that it is impossible to gauge in advance the yoke of the mitzvos in daily life until one actually experiences it. This is especially true since he also admits that the initial cause was not for its own sake, and consequently, he is biased in his own estimation that he will actually fulfill everything mentioned.

Therefore, the most honest advice is that the beginning should be his conduct in daily life according to the Shulchan Aruch for a significant period of time. During this time, they (he and the young woman) should not distract one another through meetings, etc., and it would be best if they were not in the same city. After this period of trial, he will be able to decide for himself whether he will truly live a life according to the religion of Moshe and Yisrael, or, etc.

It would be good if, in the meantime, efforts were made for the young woman to travel to Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt and established), etc. The explanation for this, as mentioned, is so that each of them can gauge for themselves the truth of their intentions in this matter.

As per your request, the letter is being sent without waiting for the queue.

With respect and blessings.

## 8894

8894

B"H, 30 Tishrei, Erev Rosh Chodesh Marcheshvan, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings!

As we stand on the day of the union of the seventh month—the month that is "satiated" (meshuba) with all good—and the month of Marcheshvan, the month of the descent of rain for blessing, for life, and for satiety;

And since the rains and their descent are the beginning, the head, and the source of all blessings, and their descent and drawing down below [is the conduit for this];

May it be the will [of Hashem] that the good writing and sealing of every man and woman of Bnei Yisrael, within the entirety of Klal Yisrael, which took place in the seventh month, be continued below in a manner that is good, visible, and revealed to us, in all that is needed by each and every one, both materially and spiritually.

And as the seal of the festivals of the seventh month, the time of our rejoicing—

With blessings for good tidings.

## 8895

[Beginning of Mar Cheshvan 5725]

Regarding your note on the Sages’ statement that "the deceased is not forgotten from the heart": 1) Thirty days (Moed Katan 8a); 2) Twelve months (Berachos 58b).

The matter itself proves that in Moed Katan, the discussion concerns the forgetting of the grief, while in Berachos, it concerns the forgetting of the person.

[And based on this, one can explain: a) Why specifically regarding this there must be a **decree** that one should forget (Rashi, Vayeishev 37:35). b) The variation in the aforementioned statement of the Sages, where in Berachos the word **"ela"** (but/only) is used—which retracts the previous statement—due to the nature of the matter.]

However, I have always wondered why a distinction is not made regarding the above concerning the **obligation** of mourning (grief—and this does not relate to "grieving" (Rashi, Sukkah 25a, s.v. *tirda*), which one is not obligated to do). It is twelve months.

And may we conclude with good.

***

**8895**

The letter is to R' Hillel Medalia, and it was written on the margin of his letter dated the eve of 26 Tishrei 5725. Additional letters to him are found above, Vol. 20, 7612, and in the references noted in the footnotes there.

**Regarding your note on the Sages’ statement:** In his letter: "As a sign of special affection, I will note: In Moed Katan (8a) we learned: 'A person should not stir up [his grief]... thirty days before the festival,' and we read there: 'What is the reason for thirty days? Rav Chisda said in the name of Rav Yehuda in the name of Rav: It happened that... and Shmuel said: Because the deceased is not forgotten from the heart for thirty days... and even if he does it for free [it is forbidden].' End quote.

"Seemingly, it is difficult to understand why Rav is not concerned that one might come to eulogize during the festival even if he does it for free? And there is also the difficulty raised by Tosafos.

"My father, of blessed memory, said that Rav follows his own reasoning in Berachos (58b): 'The deceased is not forgotten from the heart until after twelve months...' If so, there is no difference whether it is for this festival or another, and therefore, if he does it for free, it is permitted, and it is only forbidden because of the reason of the 'incident' mentioned in Moed Katan."

## 8896

B"H, 6 Marcheshvan, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the distinguished, G-d-fearing, learned, and upright individual, who seeks the good of his people, a man of noble character, a scion of a noble lineage, R' Shneur Zalman, shlita,

Greetings and blessings!

I have just seen the report in the newspaper that your brother, shlita, has received permission to leave his country and settle in Eretz Yisrael, t.w. It seems this is a continuation of the news you wrote to me about in your previous letter. I wish to offer you a *mazal tov*; the joy of every individual Jew is the joy of the entire collective, especially since one Jewish soul is like an entire world—which is one of the reasons why man was created as a single individual.

May it be the will of Hashem that from the redemption of the individual we reach the redemption of the collective, and that the departure of your brother, shlita, will be a prelude to the opening of the gates of that country—a wide-open opening, and soon, through the mercies of Hashem, for they have not ceased.

And from one piece of good news to another: good news from here, that at long last the book *Maamarei Admur HaZaken - 5762* has come off the press, and it has been sent to you separately.

I believe that when I showed you the manuscript, I remarked that it is a unique manuscript: the *maamarim* are those of the Alter Rebbe, the handwriting is that of the Mitteler Rebbe, and it was in the possession of the Tzemach Tzedek, who attested that the handwriting is that of the Mitteler Rebbe and also inserted glosses in several places. All of this testifies to the authenticity of the contents, etc. To the point that, were I not hesitant and afraid to express a definitive opinion on matters of *Chassidus*—and all the more so regarding the *maamarim* of the Alter Rebbe, of whom even the Tzemach Tzedek, whose heart was like the heart of a lion, said: "Just as there is a difference between the Holy of Holies and mundane matters, so too are his ways higher than our ways" (*Tzemach Tzedek, Yoreh Deah, Siman 63, Ot 8*), and he was speaking there regarding the *nigleh* of Torah, and certainly regarding the *pnimiyus* of Torah—I would say that the transcriptions of several *maamarim* in the book *Maamarei Admur HaZaken - 5762* are more accurate and superior to the transcriptions of the *maamarim* printed in *Likutei Torah*, even though those were printed in the days of the Tzemach Tzedek and apparently under his editorship. Perhaps at the time of the printing of *Likutei Torah*, this manuscript volume was not in the possession of the Tzemach Tzedek.

My previous letters were surely received in due time, as was the *Sefer HaSichos 5702* which I handed over via Rabbi David Asaf of Haifa, who was visiting here and has returned to Eretz Yisrael, t.w.

Incidentally, although I was concerned about your time, knowing your preoccupations, and my heart hesitated to entrust the book to you personally via Rabbi Asaf, I could not refuse his request. Especially after he showed me that the combination "Chabad"—*Chochma, Binah, Daas* together—is found in the words of the great teacher, the Rambam, in his book *Mishneh Torah, Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah (Chapter 2, Halacha 2)*—

A *halacha* that rules definitively that one is obligated to study the foundations of Torah through *hisbonenus*—through *Chochma, Binah, Daas*. Moreover, this relates to the actual performance of *mitzvos*. From his clear and explicit words there: "What is the path to His love and fear (of Hashem)? At the time when a person contemplates... and sees from them His wisdom... and he desires with a great desire to know the Great Hashem."

The absolute precision of the Rambam in his aforementioned book is well known, especially when speaking of the foundations of Torah. From this, it is understood that his phrasing, "What is the path," excludes the possibility of arriving at the love and fear of Hashem through any other way. He preceded this (the Rambam) by stating that love and fear are commandments from the Torah, and as the commentary explains there, they are also the foundations for the fulfillment of the *mitzvos*.

With respect and blessing.

## 8897

8897

B"H, 9 Marcheshvan, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To Mr. A. Ron, may he live,

Peace and Blessing!

I hereby acknowledge with thanks the receipt of your letter dated the 5th of Cheshvan, in which you write regarding the differences of opinion between the administrators of two schools in Jerusalem.

I have sent a telegram to Rabbi Zevin, shlita, and requested that, with the participation of our friends who are knowledgeable in the field of education and who are located there and personally acquainted with both administrators, etc., they should mediate between them and arrange the matter in the best possible way. I am confident that they will not delay in discussing the matter with the appropriate attention and will find the correct solution for the benefit of the students (for, after all, they are the main thing), as well as for the benefit of the administration, etc. Furthermore—and this is also essential—it should be done in a way that there is agreement from both administrators. In the field of education in particular, it is of the utmost importance that all those working in it do so with satisfaction and full agreement, rather than by way of compulsion, since the student is sensitive and reacts not only to things and matters that are overt, but also to the feelings of the teacher and educator, their mood, their attitude, and so on. Surely for someone like yourself, there is no need to elaborate.

It was pleasant for me to see in your letter your interest in the aforementioned schools, and from this I infer by way of *gezeirah shavah* regarding your interest in the other schools as well. This is an opportunity for me to express to you special thanks for bringing the issue of the two aforementioned schools to my attention, especially since I interpret this as an attitude of sympathy and loyal friendship toward Chabad and its activities. And according to the instruction of our Sages of blessed memory to elevate everything to something good, I hope that in this regard, too, there will be even more increase. My heartfelt thanks in advance, in addition to the essential point that the reward for a mitzvah is the mitzvah itself.

From one matter to another in the same vein, although I do not know if this falls within the scope of your activities: it concerns the school in Manchat, regarding which they have written to me that there is a thought and a suggestion to eliminate the higher grades there (in order to transfer them to the asbestos structures). Although, naturally, from across the sea it is difficult to be familiar with the aforementioned problem in all its details, surely the population in Manchat will grow in the near future. Therefore, on the contrary, the time demands expanding the current educational framework so that it can also absorb the children of the new settlers. After all, the increase in population is not limited to the beginning of the school year; any time of the year is the time for it.

I will take the liberty of enclosing herewith a copy of my general letter for this Rosh Hashanah, which I hope will interest you. The connection to what was written above—if one were to say that there must be a connection—is also simple: it is that according to the content of the aforementioned letter, you have the ability to accomplish much more in the two matters mentioned here than you might imagine.

With respect and blessing.

## 8899

8899

By the Grace of G-d
14 MarCheshvan, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Rabbi, G-d-fearing, etc., who engages in communal needs, etc.
Rabbi Sh. Y. (may he live and be well)

Greetings and Blessings,

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter of the 14th of MarCheshvan, along with the enclosure. I have also enclosed a copy of my letter regarding the aforementioned matter (as well as regarding the Menachem school, which I am certain is also known to you).

Regarding the committee that was chosen, due to the matters of the month of Tishrei, the confirmation of the receipt of the notification was delayed until now.

Regarding the practical aspect: what troubles me is that one of the committee members is the brother of one of the administrators. On the face of it, this is not appropriate. Even though, as I knew him when he was here, he is an upright person, nevertheless, etc. Furthermore, there is another point of wonder (a fundamental one, perhaps even more so), which is that I did not see your name on the committee. Even though it is certainly not your role to involve yourself in the selection of teachers, etc. [especially according to my own position, that the *mitzvah* one is most careful with is the *Encyclopedia Talmudit* alone, as I have written this many times], nevertheless, in my opinion, you should be on the committee as a deciding vote, in the event that the other four committee members cannot reach a consensus. Regarding these two difficulties, the one resolves the other in a manner of pleasantness and honor. I shall rely on your insight as to whether it is necessary to reveal that the aforementioned comment came from me, or if it is more diplomatic that the suggestion for the change not come from here. The reasons mentioned are obvious and there is no need to bring them from afar—from across the sea—to...

Regarding your inquiry concerning my comment on the Rambam’s *Peirush HaMishnayos*, *Demai* (Chapter 1, Mishnah 2), which you suggest is resolved by the fact that the Rambam crossed out the text: This was not a slip of the pen or the like, but rather a lapse of time (between the writing and the crossing out), as it appears, since **all** printings of the *Mishnayos* contain precisely the version of the first Mishnah, and the *Peirush HaMishnayos* was studied in that manner. I have not yet found anyone who has raised this point—that is to say, according to the aforementioned version, the Rambam’s opinion in his *Peirush HaMishnayos* is the same as Rashi’s opinion on this [and as it is resolved according to Rashi from the verse "And I have eaten of the sin-offering today," it is likewise resolved according to the Rambam].

The matter that brought me to all of the above is not to distinguish between an *onen* and "one whose deceased lies before him" regarding "I have eaten of my mourning." For even though in the *Yad HaChazakah* he mixes them regarding an *onen*, *Maaser Sheni* and *Kodshim* together, and the verses together as well, on the face of it, this is not at all definitive—for *Maaser Sheni* is not *Kodshim*. And the fact that the Rambam brings the verse regarding mourning is, as is known, the rule that he brings the simpler *derashah*. And then one must reconcile this with what is brought in the *Acharonim* that *onen* and *aveilus* do not meet together, for *aveilus* begins after the *onen* status has passed, i.e., at the burial. It occurred to me to say that according to our version in the *Peirush HaMishnayos*\*, *Maaser Sheni* is different in this regard, as its prohibition is from "I have eaten of my mourning," whereas *Kodshim* is from "I have eaten of the sin-offering today." And the fact that he does not bring this distinction explicitly in the *Yad HaChazakah* is in accordance with the Rambam’s rules, as he frequently does not write new laws if they were not explicitly brought in the *Shas*.

With respect and blessing,

**M. Schneerson**

***

\*) And it is worth noting the *Peirush HaRosh* (end of *Maaser Sheni*)—though it is possible to say that he holds entirely like Rashi (also regarding *Kodshim*).

## 8801

8801

B"H, 20 Marcheshvan, 5725
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, G-d-fearing, learned, and diligent in communal needs, R' Shmuel, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

I hereby confirm receipt of your letter from Tuesday, along with the enclosure, and the receipt is enclosed herewith.

The *pidyon nefesh* will be read at a time of favor at the holy *tziyun* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

Thank you for your note regarding the source for the words of the Alter Rebbe in his *Shulchan Aruch* concerning the time the *neshamah kedoshah* enters a person, in which you point to *Sefer HaChinuch* in the roots of the *mitzvah* of *milah*.

Although one does not offer a rebuttal to a *drush*, the matter of the entry of the *neshamah kedoshah* is relevant to *halacha*, as written in the Alter Rebbe’s *Shulchan Aruch* there. Yet, in *Sefer HaChinuch*, it is implied that, on the contrary, the time of *milah* is not specifically timed to the entry of the *neshamah*, based on the two roots he writes:

1) That the *milah* serves to distinguish them in the form of their bodies (which occurs at the time of the *milah*), just as they are distinguished from them in the form of their *neshamos* (past tense, relative to the time of *milah*).

2) To hint that just as the form of the body is completed through it (at the time of the *milah*), so too it is within his power to complete the form of his *neshamah* through the rectitude of his actions (a long time after this, when he becomes obligated in the performance of *mitzvos*, and *kal v'chomer*).

One should also note the reasons for the *mitzvah* of *milah* according to the Rambam in *Moreh Nevuchim*, Part 3, end of Chapter 49.

I was pleased to read at the end of your letter that the printing of your book is progressing. May it be His will that you soon celebrate the completion of these volumes, and that you continue to go from strength to strength as commanded by our holy Torah—and indeed, the command of the Torah is also the granting of the strength to fulfill it.

With respect and blessing.

***

8801

## 8803

8803

B"H, 21 Mar-Cheshvan, 5725
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

It appears that the dispute regarding... [name] is still not settled, and this compels me to express my deepest regret over the matter. My regret is even greater—perhaps even more so—because it seems that someone is influencing you to view this as a matter of "let the law pierce the mountain," as if great success depends on the resolution being one way, and the opposite of success, etc., if the resolution is another way. Naturally, this consumes much of your time, energy, and attention that should be directed toward what needs to be accomplished, etc.

You surely recognize and have seen tangibly how you have succeeded in a way that transcends the natural order... And even though it is certain that even then, not everything was peaceful, calm, and quiet—for we are in this world, and as my father-in-law, the Rebbe, said, since the sin of the Tree of Knowledge, there is no good without [the mixture of evil]—yet suddenly, you are giving great importance to a matter that has little importance, or perhaps even less than that.

In general, I do not involve myself in the importance of matters, as I am not on the scene. However, it is understood and simple that when there is a committee of several people—a committee appointed before a certain event—it is impossible to nullify its decision. Even if the committee’s decision does not appear correct to one side, one must not undermine the committee’s authority. After all, there is no other way to manage affairs in which several individuals are involved without having some body to mediate between them and decide where there are differences of opinion—a decision [according to Torah] of "follow the majority."

If this is true for everyone, it is all the more so regarding you. Every hour or half-hour that you spend on this dispute could be utilized effectively, and even more so, to increase activities in the schools, which Divine Providence has granted you the merit and success to direct. That half-hour or hour would be multiplied in the number and success of the students—boys and girls—of the schools...

My hope is strong that there is no need to elaborate on all that has been said, and you will gladden me with the good news that you have completely diverted your attention from the entire matter, and with joy and a glad heart, you are continuing your holy work of education on the purity of holiness for the sons and daughters of Yisrael. Regarding each one of them, it is said, "You are children to Hashem your G-d," and they are known to have a special affection. As I heard from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, the saying of the Baal Shem Tov: that Hashem’s love for every son and daughter of Yisrael is infinitely greater than the love of elderly parents for an only son born to them in their old age.

May it be the will [of Hashem] that from here on, there will be no need for letters such as these.

## 8,805

B”H, 24 Menachem Av, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of the 15th of Menachem Av,

At an auspicious time, I will mention you and your wife, may she live, at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, for the fulfillment of your heart’s desires for good, in accordance with the contents of your letter.

Given the tone of your letter, it is surely unnecessary to emphasize the necessity of conducting your daily life in a manner consistent with the instructions of our Torah—the Torah of life—and the fulfillment of its mitzvos, regarding which it is said, "and he shall live by them." I only wish to add that in matters of Torah and mitzvos, no matter how good the situation may be, there is always room to increase, and we have been promised (in such contexts) by our Sages, "Whoever adds, they add to him" (that which he requires).

Since your work involves training teachers, this is a great merit, along with a great responsibility—what you refer to in your letter as "work," and in the terminology of our Sages as "the work of Heaven." The inner point is to instill in the students the fear of Hashem and the love of Hashem, especially since they (the students) are preparing to be teachers themselves; this means that every improvement and addition within them is multiplied many times over in their future students.

It is appropriate that you have your tefillin checked, as well as the mezuzos in your home, to ensure they are all kosher according to the law. Your wife, may she live, should observe the good custom of the virtuous daughters of Israel to set aside coins for tzedakah before lighting the candles every Erev Shabbos and Erev Yom Tov.

With a request for your forgiveness, I must add that when there is sometimes a delay in matters such as these, it is often because there is a lack of full *hiddur* (meticulousness) and caution regarding the laws and statutes of *taharas hamishpachah* (niddah, *hefsek taharah*, immersion in a kosher mikveh, etc.) due to an incomplete knowledge of the details. Therefore, you should clarify this with a Rav who is a *moreh hora’ah* (halachic authority) to ensure that there is no deficiency in your knowledge of these matters.

With respect and blessings for good tidings in all the above.

## 8807

8807

B"H, 27 MarCheshvan, 5725

Brooklyn, N.Y.

Mr. P. Yosef, may he live

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 9 MarCheshvan, in which you write about how you first became acquainted with the Chabad movement back in Holland, and regarding your current place of study, where you find it difficult at times to witness the inappropriate attitude toward mitzvos on the part of those who have studied more than you have.

My hope is strong—based on what our Sages of blessed memory taught us, that action is the essential thing—that this feeling of yours, the distress you feel at seeing the aforementioned attitude, will be brought to the attention of those you write about in a way that will influence them to change their attitude. After all, we have been promised that words that come from the heart enter the heart and accomplish their purpose. This is especially true when these words are accompanied by a living example from your daily life—a life of Torah and mitzvos. There is nothing that can stand before the will; if in every place the mitzvah of "love your fellow as yourself" is great, as it is a fundamental principle of the Torah, to show the path of Torah and mitzvos to those who are mistaken and confused, then all the more so in the land upon which the eyes of Hashem your G-d are from the beginning of the year until the end of the year. This is particularly true for those who are studying at an institution for instructors and educators, who will be tasked with influencing many students. It is well known that in order to influence students in a certain field, it is necessary for the influencer to possess that quality to the greatest possible degree.

I hope you have seen my general letter for Rosh Hashanah, which was published in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established, in the Holy Tongue. One of the central points in that letter is how even a single individual can accomplish great and wondrous things in matters of goodness if only they utilize all their strengths as is fitting.

I trust you are aware of the Chitas (Chumash, Tehillim, Tanya) study cycles, which are accessible to every soul, and that you fulfill them every day.

Blessings for success and for good tidings.

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

P.S. Since we write here in the Holy Tongue, English, and Yiddish, please be so kind as to inform us in which language to write to you in the future.

## 8,908

[Late MarCheshvan, 5725]

Regarding the order of life when the husband studies Torah for a portion of his day (at the very least) — it is understood that **this is** the correct beginning, and this is how it should be among Bnei Yisrael [since the wedding is the beginning of a "building of eternity"]. However, it is also understood that this is connected with limitation and measurement, and a certain degree of Tzimtzum regarding physicality. Since this is felt in daily life, and on the other hand, the life of Bnei Yisrael must be — as it is written — with joy, goodness of heart, and confidence —

It follows that there must be an agreement on such an order of life — **from the outset, a full agreement, and with joy** (that they are beginning their lives with the life of Torah, the Torah of life).

And if both of them agree to this, and in the manner mentioned above, may it be Hashem’s will that all of this should be with success, in a good and auspicious time.

## 8819

*[Late Marcheshvan 5725]*

In response to your questions from some time ago: The three aspects of milah (the action, the state of being circumcised, and not being uncircumcised) do not constitute **three** separate mitzvos. Regarding the determination of whether this is a mitzvah dependent on time (*zman grama*) or not—this must be applied to the **mitzvah itself** (which is one) and not to its individual details. Furthermore, there is no time (from the eighth day onward) when one is not obligated in this mitzvah.

- The concept of *bechirah* (choice) applies only to one who possesses *sechel* (intellect). For one who lacks *sechel* acts out of nature or the like, or does not act at all; however, it is not applicable to say in such a case that he **sees** two **equal** options and **chooses** one of them. Just as one cannot say that a blind person **chooses** one color from among several colors. Rather, this person of *sechel* is in a state where his *sechel* does not **compel** him to choose a specific color (logic), but merely presents the colors (logics) before him.

## 8819

[Late MarCheshvan 5725]

## 8811

8811

B"H, Rosh Chodesh Kislev, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter.

As we stand at the beginning of the month of Kislev, the month of miracles and redemption, may it be the will of Hashem that just as in those days, at this time—so too shall it be in these our days, at this time; both regarding the *ner mitzvah* and *Torah Ohr* (the concept of Chanukah) and, in particular, regarding the dissemination of the inner dimension of the Torah, the *Torah* of Chassidus (the concept of the 19th of Kislev and the 10th of Kislev).

May all of this be in a permanent manner, as our Sages said regarding the Chanukah lights: "The lights shall always shine toward the face of the Menorah."

With blessings for good tidings in all the aforementioned,

**M. Schneerson**

#### ***8811***

General details. Printed in *Likkutei Sichos*, Vol. 18, p. 216 (the date there requires correction), and completed according to a photocopy of the letter.

**At the beginning of the month:** Which is the head (that includes) all the days of the month (*Ateres Rosh*, Gate of Rosh Hashanah, Ch. 2).

**The concept of Chanukah:** That they did not "allow them to engage in *Torah* and *mitzvos*" (Rambam, beginning of *Hilchos Chanukah*), and the miracle and its publicizing through the candle and light.

**Inner dimension of the Torah:** For this is the "way of life" mentioned at the end of the verse (Mishlei 6:23), "For the *ner mitzvah* and *Torah Ohr*" (see *Sotah* 21a; *Kuntres Eitz Chayim* at the beginning and Ch. 15).

**19th of Kislev:** When the primary dissemination of the *maayanos* (wellsprings) outward began (*Toras Shalom*, 19th of Kislev, 5668).

**As our Sages said:** *Bamidbar*, and as explained by the Ramban at the beginning of *Parshas Beha'aloscha*.

**The lights... shall shine:** The souls of Israel shall always have their hearts directed toward the inner dimension of the Divine Will (*Likutei Torah*, *D'H Beha'aloscha*, end of Ch. 6. Based on this, it is resolved how the concept of "toward the face" applies to the Chanukah lights).

## 8817

[Chanukah 5725]

...It is the custom for poor youths to go around to the doors, etc...

Magen Avraham, beginning of the Laws of Chanukah—cited in Divrei Nechemiah—an addition to the Shulchan Aruch of the Alter Rebbe, end of section 670. However, this still requires further study, as I have not seen any hint of this in the Chanukah customs of my father-in-law, the Rebbe. And even though "we have not seen" is not proof—especially regarding tzedakah, where "giving in secret is beautiful"—it is, on the face of it, significant that (aside from what is explained in the maamar beginning *Be-Chaf-Hey Be-Kislev*—in *Torah Or* and its explanations in *Shaarei Orah* and *Ohr HaTorah*—that the act of tzedakah is called a "helmet of salvation," and the mesiras nefesh of Chanukah drew down a great gift, the drawing down of the "helmet of salvation" to be, etc.) it has not occurred to anyone to mention in any maamar Chassidus (that I have seen thus far) or sicha or letter of our Rebbes regarding tzedakah specifically on Chanukah (even though **many** matters of Chanukah were mentioned and explained therein, and in detail). Especially since this [custom] is found in several [communities of] Poland, etc. And whoever finds anything to this effect in the teachings of our Nesi'im will certainly inform me, and I thank him in advance from the depths of my heart. In the responsa of Maharid (and perhaps this segment is from the Tzemach Tzedek), it explains the above: that on Chanukah, one distributes "money to the poor who go around to the doors, which is at the entrance of the house from the outside, which is the aspect of 'with all your might,' 'with all your money.'"

#### 8817

Printed in *Shaarei Halacha U'Minhag*, Vol. 2, p. 265.

## 8819

8819

B"H, Days of Chanukah, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings!

As we stand in the days of Chanukah, which follow closely upon 19 Kislev, the "Day of Good Tidings," it is appropriate to explain the common denominator between the days of Chanukah and 19 Kislev (in terms of *avodah*).

The miracle of Chanukah was with oil—which symbolizes the *pnimiyus* of Torah—for the lamps of the *Mikdash*. Their place was specifically in the *Heichal*, where not everyone could enter, and the time for lighting them was during the day.

However, in the days of Mattisyahu ben Yochanan Kohen Gadol and his sons, who sacrificed themselves for *Kiddush Hashem*—the true *yichud* in their declaration of "with all your soul," even if He takes your soul—the miracle occurred. Through this, they were commanded regarding the lamps of Chanukah, whose *mitzvah* is to be lit by each and every one (soul), at the entrance of his house from the outside (world), and specifically after sunset (year).

All these things correspond to the study of the *pnimiyus* of Torah—the "oil of Torah." Initially, it was hidden and concealed from all but the most select individuals, and even then, it was practiced with great modesty, not in public. But in these final generations, it is a *mitzvah* to reveal it—beginning in the days of the Arizal and continuing until the redemption of 19 Kislev, which initiated the essential task of spreading the *maayanos* outward, until each and every person can understand and grasp how *Hu memale kol almin* and *Hu sovev kol almin*, and so on.

May it be the will [of Hashem] that each of us accomplishes this in all three details: 1) in the manner of *hafatzah* (spreading), 2) the *maayanos* themselves, 3) until they reach even the "outside."

And may this be done with *chayus* and light, and with "increasing and growing in light."

With blessings for a happy Chanukah,

**M. Schneerson**

* * *

Regarding the note on the honor due to one's parents—**"And they said"** in Rashi (*Noach* 11:32)—this refers to the **claim of the nations of the world.** It is impossible to suggest that they would **claim** why Avraham Avinu did not fulfill the Torah before it was given, and this is well understood.

Regarding the matter of Cham, Besuel, and Lavan—there is no proof there, for they are discussed in the context of degradation, whereas in the current case, it is a matter of *shev v'al taaseh* (refraining from action) regarding honor.

## 8820

*[Late Kislev 5725]*

...I read in the newspaper that a *get* was arranged three times via telephone (from Vienna) by the husband: "Write and give," etc.

I am interested to know if this report is accurate, and what the reasoning is—for on the face of it, this requires significant study (*tzarich iyun gadol*). After all, via telephone, this is not the voice of the husband **at all**; rather, his speech triggers changes in an electric current (the nature and **essence** of which no one knows to this day, only the manner of its operation. But it is "known" that this is not at all airwaves or movements of the sort that occur during speech), and **subsequently**, these changes activate a thin membrane that the craftsman **intended** and designed in a specific way.

It is understood that this does not touch upon the matter of the authority of photography and the like regarding *agunot* and similar cases—for in those instances, there must be a sign and the knowledge of the *Beis Din*, whereas in a *get*, there must be the speech of the husband.

The same applies to the fact that there is no basis for hearing the reading of the *Megillah*, etc., via telephone or radio and the like—for this is not the **voice** of a person **at all** (not even like one who blows into a pit). It is obvious that it is a great innovation, and a strange one at that, to say that a secondary power—and a **fundamental** change in essence, etc.—can take the place of speech.

And even though there are *Acharonim*, *poskim*, and *gedolei Yisrael* [...]—it is proven from their own responsa that those who explained to them the nature and manner of the telephone's operation were fundamentally mistaken in this, and there is no need to elaborate further.

#### ***8820***

Printed in *Likkutei Sichos*, Vol. 9, p. 336.

**The same applies... Megillah:** See also *Likkutei Sichos*, Vol. 21, p. 496.

## 8821

8821

B"H, Zos Chanukah, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the congregation of Yeshurun, lovers and supporters of Torah and mitzvos,
May Hashem preserve them and grant them life,

Peace and blessing!

The preparations for the upcoming annual undertaking of the Merkaz Yeshivos Tomchei Temimim Lubavitch (on the 21st of Shevat, upcoming) are in full swing. I would like to hope that every single person, for whom the matter of Torah and mitzvos is near and dear, will certainly mobilize immediately to contribute his (and her) share to the success of this annual undertaking.

All Jewish Yomim Tovim and days of remembrance, by bringing fresh life into the daily routine of the present, connect the Jewish future with the Jewish past. This is also true regarding the days of Chanukah, as is specifically emphasized in the text of the *Hoda'ah* (thanksgiving prayer): **"In those days, at this time."**

Chanukah reminds us of the attempt by the external world to penetrate the internal Jewish world—not so much through an effort to eradicate Torah and mitzvos entirely, as through a striving to empty Torah and mitzvos of their inner spirit of *kedushah* and *Elokus*. This is emphasized in the Chanukah prayer: the Greeks strove **"to make them forget Your Torah"**—to make Jews forget **Your** (Divine) Torah, **"and to lead them away from the laws of Your will"**—and to lead them away from the laws of **Your** (Divine) will. The Torah as a "wisdom," or as a "literary masterpiece," etc., was not in danger; nor were the so-called "ethical" and "rational" mitzvos. What was placed in danger was the deep Jewish *emunah* in **Toras Hashem**, the **Ribono shel Olam's** Torah, **Torah min HaShamayim**, and **mitzvos Hashem**, the **Ribono shel Olam's** mitzvos. This danger is just as serious today as it was then; the "Greeks" of today and their Jewish collaborators and imitators, the "Hellenists," are no less dangerous to Jewish existence than those of the past.

Therefore, it is so important today to strengthen and expand an institution such as the Lubavitcher Yeshiva, where the study of Torah is permeated with *kedushah*, *yiras shamayim*, and *hiddur mitzvah*, and above all—with a sense of responsibility, *ahavas Yisrael*, and *mesiras nefesh*, like the Chashmonaim of old, to go out into the street to spread the **ner mitzvah** and **Torah Ohr**, more and more, in accordance with the instruction of the **ner Chanukah**.

It is to be hoped that the *askanim* of the Yeshiva and the lovers of Torah and mitzvos in general will utilize the few weeks remaining until the annual dinner to strengthen their efforts and their participation, also in an increasing measure, to ensure the financial and spiritual success of the annual undertaking. In this merit, every one of the workers and supporters will be privileged to receive a **teshuah gedolah** in everything they need, for themselves and their families, may they live, in both *gashmius* and *ruchnius* together.

With respect and blessing for success and good tidings,

**M. Schneerson**

## 8822

8822

B"H, Zos Chanukah, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the Editorial Board of "Di Yiddishe Heim,"
May Hashem preserve them and grant them life,

Blessings and peace!

On the occasion of the five-year jubilee of "Di Yiddishe Heim," I send my greetings and blessing to the esteemed members of the editorial board, as well as to the esteemed readers of this meritorious publication.

Throughout the years of its publication, "Di Yiddishe Heim," both through its content and in other respects, has justified its name as a truly Torah-true publication for the Jewish home; a publication that strives to be in harmony with the spirit of a Jewish home—as a Jewish home ought to be—and to strengthen and enrich this spirit, the spirit of purity and holiness, and to illuminate the Jewish home with the *ner mitzvah* and *Torah Ohr*.

And one should not convince oneself that it is difficult to conduct a Jewish home as it ought to be. On the contrary—in truth, it is difficult for a Jew, by virtue of his entire essence as a Jew, to conduct himself otherwise, because everything that is foreign, contrary to the spirit of *Torah* and *mitzvos*, is in opposition to his inner nature and inner being. As the Alter Rebbe, *Baal HaTanya* and *Shulchan Aruch*, expressed it: A Jew neither can nor wants to be severed from *Elokus*.

This fundamental perspective is also clearly brought out in the *Rambam* (*Sefer Nashim*, Laws of Divorce, end of Chapter 2), in connection with the *halacha* that when one compels a Jew to perform a *mitzvah*, he performs the *mitzvah* with his **own will.** Superficially, there appears to be a contradiction here. However, the *Rambam* explains that **every** Jew wants to be a Jew and wants to perform all the *mitzvos* and distance himself from *aveiros*—it is only due to false rationalizations that he thinks he does not want to. The compulsion, therefore, is merely to remove the constraint of these false rationalizations, at which point his true will to perform the *mitzvah* freely and willingly is liberated.

The task of "Di Yiddishe Heim" is to help the readers understand more deeply and better how and what a truly Jewish home should be; to earn and justify this name to the fullest extent, so that it may be a home for the Divine *Shechinah*, as the *Ribono shel Olam* promised: **"And I will dwell among them"**—I will rest among them, in every Jew and in every Jewish home.

And where the *Shechinah* rests, there also rests blessing and success, both materially and spiritually.

According to the instruction of our Sages in the *Mishnah* (*Avos*, end of Chapter 5), five years is a period that demonstrates the fruits of success in the realm of influence and education. May the *Ribono shel Olam* help that the success of "Di Yiddishe Heim" in its first five years serve as a sign of blessing and success for the future, and in an increasing measure, as is appropriate in these days—specifically highlighted by the *mitzvah* of *ner Chanukah*: every night of *Chanukah* another candle must be added, which reminds us that we must constantly spread the *ner mitzvah* and *Torah Ohr* in an increasing measure.

And as my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, says: "One must listen to what the candles are telling us."

With blessings for success—and in a manner of—*mosif v'holech v'or* (increasing and going on in light),

**M. Schneerson**

***

**Alter Rebbe... expressed it:** See also above, Vol. 20, Letter 7600, and the references in the notes there.

**Halacha:** Laws of Divorce.

**"And I will dwell among them"... every Jew:** Cited in *Shelah*, Parshas Terumah, and elsewhere.

**My father-in-law, the Rebbe... says:** See also above, Vol. 14, Letter 4839, and the references in the notes there.

## 8923

8923

B"H, 11 Teves, 5725

To Dr. Shlomo Kodesh, may he live and be well,

I hereby acknowledge with many thanks the receipt of your letter from the 20th of Kislev, as well as the books you have published. Certainly, if you have further publications, please send them to our library, which is for the benefit of the many, and thank you in advance.

As is the Jewish custom to connect matters with the affairs of the day, and since your letter arrived during the days of Chanukah, it follows that the lesson of the Chanukah lights is to be "increasing and growing" in light and illumination. As is explained in many places, the Chanukah light is a symbol of "the mitzvah is a lamp and the Torah is light," and also "the lamp of Hashem is the soul of man." Thus, whoever increases in the lamp of mitzvah and the light of Torah, is granted an increase in all that they require, from the soul down to the physical body as well.

And based on what you wrote regarding your ancestral roots, any elaboration on the aforementioned for someone like yourself is surely unnecessary.

With respect and blessing,

Regarding the interview, it has been passed on to the secretariat, and they will surely be in touch with you.

8824

[23 Teves, 5725]

I see it as my duty to emphasize the great breach that exists in the area of the matters discussed in these sections of the Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer, due to a lack of knowledge regarding this among the masses of the Jewish people. Therefore, it is certain that by publicizing the gravity of the matter, many will become cautious and make the necessary corrections. I would like to suggest several points for their attention:

(a) Regarding prostate surgery, almost all physicians sever (or disconnect in another manner) several of the seminal ducts; furthermore, they begin this (the disconnection of the ducts) even before the surgery itself. In my discussions with several rabbis who are *morei hora’ah* (halachic authorities), they were unaware that there is room for a question here. And even though the first volume of *Otzar HaPoskim* touches upon the aforementioned at its end, because it appears in the notes section and they did not establish a dedicated section for this subject—but rather grouped it with other matters—very few are aware of the halachic discourse (*shakla v’tarya*) found there. Even those who are aware do not have a clear conclusion regarding the practical application.

It requires study whether most surgeons would agree not to sever the seminal ducts at all, but certainly most would agree that the disconnection could come at the conclusion of the procedure. However, in order for the matter to be publicized, a conclusion must be reached and presented in a dedicated section—not at the end of a volume amidst various other topics, but in an appropriate place, and perhaps also by including a reference in the general index and the subject index, etc.

Furthermore, if surgeons were to provide testimony that they performed the aforementioned treatment without severing the seminal ducts and were successful, it is highly probable that this would influence many other physicians not to sever them at all. Surely, even for the sake of a *safek sfeika* (a double doubt), the publicity is worthwhile.

(b) Similar to the above and in continuation of it, there are many cases where one testicle is found to be out of place, and doctors treat it by bringing it down into the scrotum. There is also the treatment to examine where the second testicle is located, etc. Sometimes in these cases, they perform surgery, and the method of surgery is often linked to the Torah prohibition of *kerus shofcha* (crushed or severed testicles), etc., even though the treatment could potentially be performed in a different manner—and in many cases, in a manner that is permissible *lechatchila* (from the outset). Yet, the parents do not know that there is room for doubt and for asking a rabbi. What is even more concerning is that many *morei hora’ah* are unaware of these details. Here, too, publicity in the next volume in an appropriate place will certainly help prevent a stumbling block.

All of the above also applies to the treatment of a hernia in the scrotum, with the addition that this concerns not only children but also adults, etc.

(c) There is another issue, which is closely related and fundamental, and furthermore, the problem grows from time to time: the question of child adoption. The number of those seeking to adopt children is constantly increasing. As I have seen in many cases of couples who are meticulous even regarding the slightest detail of the words of the Sages, they **pay no attention at all** to the fact that all the laws of *yichud* (seclusion), hugging, and kissing, etc., remain in full force regarding adopted children. I have seen in several books and pamphlets that deal with the problem of how to ensure that a brother does not marry his sister, etc., and there are those who discuss the question of inheritance, etc.; but all of this comes after many years of adoption, and they are in the category of *safek* (doubt), and many are only in matters of monetary law. In contrast, the aforementioned prohibitions (*yichud*, etc.) are in the category of *vadai* (certainty) and begin many years before the children reach maturity.

It is simple that the above is not comparable to the matter of raising an orphan, which was common among the Jewish people. In these years, the intention of the adopters is to conceal from the adopted child that they are not his true parents, and they strive and are precise not to show any movement or matter that would arouse suspicion in the heart of the adopted child that the relationship toward him is not similar to the relationship toward his peers from their own parents. Furthermore, many of the institutions that distribute children for adoption receive (or even demand) a promise in advance that the child will grow up without any complications or disturbances—meaning that the relationship toward him in all details will be like a son or daughter born of their own flesh and blood. They add that doctors and psychologists insist that such a relationship is necessary so that the upbringing will be without complications. Certainly, for those such as yourselves, the length is unnecessary, and I only add that the number of adopted children is **many** times greater than what the statisticians estimate or report, since many of the adopters, and even more so many of those who give their children up for adoption, do not want any publicity for various reasons.

Therefore, all the publicity and all the warnings regarding the gravity of the matter, etc., that will be included in the next volume—would that it suffice to bring about the correction that must ultimately come in the aforementioned matter. And although, on the face of it, the place for such publicity should be in a book read by laymen and those who wish to adopt children, the beginning of this should come in a book such as *Otzar HaPoskim* and the like, with appropriate emphasis on the prohibition and that the prohibition is transgressed every single day. In the conditions of our times, it is almost impossible to be cautious of it except through strict and constant effort, even if the adopters of the children are G-d-fearing.

It is simple that it is in the hands of the *Otzar HaPoskim* editorial board, as a compendium for *morei hora’ah* in Israel, to demand from monthlies, weeklies, and periodicals to publicize the aforementioned matter, and there is room to say that they will fulfill their request and demand in this.

With respect and with a blessing that Hashem will grant success to your holy work to teach the people of Hashem the "King's Way"—which is the way of the Holy One, blessed be He—in their daily lives, and as mentioned above, in matters whose consequences reach until the end of all generations.

**M. Schneerson**

## 8,926

8,926

B"H, 25 Teves, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Dr. S. Friedlander, may he live and be well,

Peace and blessing!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your book, *Pie XII et le IIIe Reich*, and I thank you for sending it to me.

Regarding the importance of your work, it is certainly unnecessary to elaborate at length. I will only add that this importance is not merely a matter of history for future generations; it holds a special significance in our own time. For, to our great sorrow, there are still those today who use missionary tactics and fabricated stories—events that never occurred—to detach the sons and daughters of Israel from the religion of their ancestors, from a life of Torah and mitzvos. Your book arrives and not only refutes many of these claims, but proves that the very opposite is the truth.

And although, in truth, our Torah and our religion do not require proof from external sources—for it is revealed and known that the Torah is the very essence of the life of the Jewish people, and any action against it undermines the foundation of our existence, both of the individual and the collective—nevertheless, there are people and circles who, for various reasons and motives, still require proof in this regard from other fields, whether from history, archaeology, or the like.

And if one who saves a single soul is considered as if he has saved an entire world, how much more so is this true for one who influences many of our Jewish brethren in this direction and establishes them on the path of truth.

I see on the cover that you are planning to prepare further work on this same period. May it be His will that success accompany you in this as well.

With respect and blessing,

It is my hope that you will send your publications—those already released and those to be released in the future—to our library for the benefit of the many, and I thank you in advance.

## 8827

8827

B"H, Rosh Chodesh Shevat, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter dated...

Regarding the significance of this day:

The Torah states: "In the eleventh month, on the first of the month, Moshe began to explain this Torah," and Rashi explains: "He explained it to them in seventy languages."

This is the *atpashuta* (extension) of Moshe in every generation for the six hundred thousand souls, though it is in a state of great concealment and hiddenness.

Furthermore, this applies to every *tzaddik* and sage who engages in *Oriyta* (Torah).

Moreover, in every generation, sparks from the soul of *Moshe Rabbeinu* descend and clothe themselves in the body and soul of the sages of the generation—the eyes of the community—to teach the people knowledge, etc., and to serve Hashem with heart and soul.

From all the above, it is understood that to teach the people knowledge and the service of Hashem, one must reach the level of "explaining in seventy languages," should there be anyone among the people who requires this.

We see this approach to learning tangibly in the conduct of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, who commanded and oversaw the translation of Torah teachings—including the teachings of Chassidus—into many languages: languages in their literal sense, as well as the "seventy languages and facets" of the one language of Torah—to explain even the esoteric part of Torah, which in our generations has been revealed in the teachings of Chassidus, to everyone, even the simple folk.

And with all this—also regarding showing them the path to serve Hashem with heart and soul.

May it be the will of Hashem that in these days, close to the day of his *hilula*, and with even greater intensity and strength on the day of the *hilula* itself—which is the tenth day of the eleventh month—everyone will be stirred to make good resolutions. These should subsequently be realized in good practice, in good deeds—which is the essential thing—to walk in his ways and paths mentioned above, a permanent and constant walk, in a manner of "victory, eternity, and forever," without any interruption whatsoever.

And may this continue and add to all the matters for which "this day is auspicious," and in the words of our Rebbe, the *Nasi*, the master of the *hilula*:

To connect to the Tree of Life, to receive—you, your wives, your sons, and your daughters—Hashem’s blessing with an abundance of life, good livelihood, and *nachas* from your descendants, and may you be blessed with children, life, and ample sustenance.

With blessings,

**M. Schneerson**

## 8,930

8,930

B"H, 4 Shevat, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

The yahrtzeit of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, is approaching. While Kfar Chabad comes to our minds frequently in general, this day certainly stirs and brings the memory of the village to the fore with even greater intensity.

This is an appropriate place for me to express once again my strong hope—even though I do not know what must be done to achieve it—that the necessary efforts will finally be made to enable Kfar Chabad to serve as a place of refuge for a significant number of the friends and brethren of the village's residents, as well as those who were their companions in distress in the past. May they be with them, B"B, and their partners in settlement in Eretz Yisrael, may it be rebuilt and established—the land upon which the eyes of Hashem your G-d are from the beginning of the year to the end of the year, and to which the eyes of our brethren, the Children of Israel, who are in distress and captivity, are also turned.

I doubt I would have written to you again regarding this matter, especially since you were among the founders of Kfar Chabad. However, the matter is proceeding with difficulty and complications that are not the norm. In this regard, if there were a great need for proof that this is a matter of holiness, perhaps there would be room for complications and difficulties (though not such as these). But it is a simple fact that the matter is holy in and of itself, in addition to the primary point that Kfar Chabad is the conduit for the spreading of the wellsprings. Furthermore, the obligations of all sides regarding the difficulties and obstacles have already been met.

May it be the will that very soon Kfar Chabad will expand and spread, even in the literal sense, with additional tracts of land, continuing to grow. And in a short time thereafter, these areas will also be built up with Chabad institutions and filled with new immigrants from our country, who will ascend from there—they, their sons, their daughters, and all that they possess—with kindness and mercy. They will continue the Chabad tradition and way of life according to the teachings of Chabad, founded upon the three loves: love of Hashem, love of the Torah, and love of Israel, in Eretz Yisrael.

With respect, appreciation, and blessing.

Surely you have received my letter from Rosh Chodesh Shevat, and my previous letter is also a chapter in the transition from narrow straits to the expanse.

Just now, they brought from the bindery something timely regarding these days, which is the second part of *Sefer HaZichronos* (Yiddish), and it is being sent to you separately.

## 8931

B"H, 4 Shevat, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To all participants in the annual dinner
For the educational institution Oholei Torah
May Hashem protect them and grant them life

Peace and Blessing!

This year’s dinner takes place on the eve of the fifteenth of Shevat, Rosh Hashanah La'ilanot (the New Year for Trees). This highlights the special connection between this undertaking and the day. For, as is well known, the Torah states that man is compared to a tree (*Ha'adam eitz hasadeh*), and through this, the Torah provides us with an important lesson and directive, especially in the field of chinuch.

When a tree is young—and even more so when it is still in the form of a seed—every bit of proper care, however small and insignificant it may seem, is something that, over time, multiplies manifold. The full results are expressed in the mature tree and the fruit it bears.

The same is true regarding the individual influence upon a child. Even an action that may seem, at the moment, to be very small—to the point where one might reconsider whether it is even worth investing effort and energy into it—can be transformed into an extraordinarily great and lasting influence on their daily conduct according to Torah and mitzvos. And as our Sages of blessed memory say, the true fruits are the mitzvos (*Mai peiri — mitzvos*). Therefore, every effort is justified in the field of chinuch, for here we are dealing with the entire future of the student and their lifelong well-being.

The educational institution **Oholei Torah** is an institution where the aforementioned approach is upheld and utilized for the benefit of the students, may they live. The institution therefore deserves a special effort on the part of the baalei-batim and friends to provide the necessary support for the maintenance and expansion of the institution, both in quantity and in quality.

It is to be hoped that all lovers of Torah and mitzvos will respond warmly to this call for help from the educational institution Oholei Torah and will provide their support with a generous hand.

I send my greetings and blessing to the esteemed guests and all the participants, may they live.

With respect and blessing for success.

## 8951

B"H, 12 Shevat, 5725
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi and Chossid, G-d-fearing and of noble lineage, etc., Moreinu HaRav Elimelech Alter, may he live:

Peace and blessing!

...Regarding our discussion concerning the debate as to whether one who eats chametz on Pesach—even though he has transgressed a Torah prohibition—nonetheless fulfills the positive mitzvah of "you shall remove" (*tashbitu*) and thereby nullifies the negative prohibition of "it shall not be seen" (*bal yeira'eh*): I found a note in my records that this is indeed the position of the *Teshuvah Me'ahavah* (section 182), contrary to the *Tzafnas Paneach* on the Rambam (Laws of Chametz u'Matzah, Chapter 1, Halacha 3). I do not have the sefer before me at this moment.

- As for the critiques of the Ra'avad and the Ramban on the *Baal HaMaor* (to the Rif, first chapter of *Pesachim*), one could interpret their view as being that while it is forbidden to eat, if one did transgress and eat, he has fulfilled the positive mitzvah, etc.

***

**Moreinu HaRav Elimelech Alter:** Panet, Brooklyn.

## 8,937

B"H, Erev Tu B'Shvat, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Dear Children, may you live,

Peace and blessing!

I received with joy your drawings, along with the letter from your teacher and ganenet, Mrs. Rachel Zamir, may she live.

The drawings and the letter reached me before the Hillula (yahrzeit) of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, and my response is being written on Erev Tu B'Shvat, the New Year for Trees. Indeed, in your drawings as well, I saw boys and girls, trees and flowers. To connect all these matters, I will remind you of the great affection and love that my father-in-law, the Rebbe, felt for all the sons and daughters of Yisrael, both the great and the small—and especially the small ones, whose education and well-being he cared for wherever they might be. In every boy and girl, he saw a sapling destined to grow and become a beautiful fruit-bearing tree, yielding good and pleasant fruits.

Of course, this depends on the desire and the avodah of the boy and the girl themselves, and the parable for this is the tree. When one plants a seed or a tender sapling, one must protect it from weeds and all kinds of pests, and provide it with water, and so on, until it grows and becomes a tree that bears good fruit. This is the same for every boy and girl, of whom Hashem Yisbarach said through His prophet that they are "the branch of My planting, the work of My hands, in which I glory."

The Creator, blessed be He, has planted within you a holy neshamah, a literal part of Elokus from above. It is your responsibility to protect this divine "seed" from weeds and pests—that is to say, from improper friends—and to provide it with living waters. And there is no water other than Torah, the Torah of life, and its mitzvos, which are life to those who perform them. Then, Hashem Yisbarach will grant and continue to grant you His blessing that you succeed in becoming "trees" that bear good and excellent fruit, to the joy of your parents and teachers, and to the pride and glory of our people, the children of Yisrael.

With blessings for success and good tidings,

**M. Schneerson**

## 8839

8839

B"H, 15 Shevat, 5725
Brooklyn.

To the Rabbi, the distinguished scholar, G-d-fearing, and esteemed individual, who occupies himself with communal needs, etc.,
Rabbi M. [Amsel], may he live.

Greetings and blessings,

In response to your letters, in which you suggest that I enter into correspondence to clarify practical halachic rulings regarding several matters you enumerated in your letters.

Surely you are aware that, in general, it is not my practice to involve myself in issuing practical halachic rulings, especially in matters where many are already debating the subject and have exhausted the matter from all its sides.

This is the path that my father-in-law, the Rebbe, charted for the Beis Harav — except in exceptional cases where there are specific reasons and important causes to enter into such halachic debate.

Furthermore, there are many in New York and in the United States in general who would be overjoyed to be offered the opportunity to provide their opinion on the aforementioned matters (I refer, of course, to Rabbis who are worthy of the mantle of a *moreh hora'ah* — to render judgment impartially and for practical application), and they would surely be grateful to you for publishing their words, etc.

I ask for your forgiveness. In addition to the primary reason mentioned above, there is also the fact that I do not have the leisure or the time to fulfill such a request.

With respect and blessing,

**Menachem Schneerson**

*Note:* One may recall the teaching of our Sages, "At a time of dispersion, gather" (Berachos 63a) — and this is the reason for the matter in which they are being dispersed.

Regarding what the aforementioned Rabbi, R. Y. M. Hakohen [Rapoport], may he live, wrote concerning *bein hashmashos* (twilight) — I was surprised by its content. Especially since I have known the aforementioned person for some time, it is difficult to understand how he could write such things (though the *Kuntres Hashlamah*, the *Sefer Zichron Yosef*, *Bnei Tzion*, *Divrei Chanah*, and *Kaneh V'kinamon* are not currently at hand). Perhaps he was motivated by a desire to defend what was written at the end of *Chidushei HaRema"l* and his colleagues — and he overlooked what was written on this in *Nimukei Orach Chaim*, siman 261. *Dai l'chachima* (a word to the wise is sufficient).

Regarding what the aforementioned wrote concerning the Alter Rebbe’s position in his *Siddur* on this matter — the Alter Rebbe did not rely on that source there, nor did he even bring it — neither the Yerushalmi nor the Radbaz. And as for his difficulty — it is impossible to say that Rabbeinu Tam is a lone opinion, since the Chida wrote in *Shem HaGedolim* that the ruling of the Shulchan Aruch is the ruling of the great scholars of his generation. (If we were to interpret the Chida’s words this way — as a general rule — he would have had to question and refute all the words of the Rema in every place where he disagrees with the Shulchan Aruch, for we have never heard that in the Beis Din of the Rema there sat two hundred and one great scholars.

In any case,) this is irrelevant to the words of the Alter Rebbe, who was precise in his language that "Rabbeinu Tam and his colleagues are a lone opinion **against** the Geonim, and those who followed them, etc." In addition to his writing that the Shulchan Aruch retracted his position. As for the Pri Chadash, the Chida, and the Aruch HaShulchan citing him — in the case at hand, **all of them** wrote in accordance with the opinion of the Geonim (the Pri Chadash is cited in the *Siddur* there; the Birkei Yosef and Aruch HaShulchan in *Orach Chaim* 261 there).

For an explanation of the aforementioned opinions, see also *Sefer Minchas Cohen*, *Sefer Bein Hashmashos* (by R. Y. M. Tukachinsky), and the entry "Bein Hashmashos" in the *Talmudic Encyclopedia*.

## 8840

8840

By the Grace of G-d
15 Shevat, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To all participants in the annual celebration of the Merkaz Yeshivos Tomchei Tmimim Lubavitch, may they live:

Peace and blessing!

The Holy Torah, which is eternal, contains guidance relevant to all events, in all times. This is especially true for an event directly connected to Torah, such as the annual celebration of the central Yeshiva, Tomchei Tmimim Lubavitch.

This year, the celebration takes place on the day we begin reading the parsha of **Mishpatim** ("Laws"), which opens with the laws of **bein adam l’chaveiro**—between man and his fellow. However, the parsha of Mishpatim follows immediately after Parshas **Yisro**, the sedra of **Matan Torah** (the Giving of the Torah), with the **Aseres HaDibros** (Ten Commandments), which begin with **"Anochi Hashem Elokecha"** ("I am Hashem your G-d"). Furthermore, the sedra concludes with the matter of the *mizbeach* (altar), which pertains to **bein adam l’Makom**—the framework of man’s obligations to G-d. As our Sages, of blessed memory, note regarding this: "The parsha of *dinim* (civil laws) is placed immediately after the parsha of the *mizbeach* to show that the Sanhedrin must be situated near the Sanctuary (the *mizbeach*)."

In the above, the fundamental principle is underscored in various and profound ways:

When it comes to the question of establishing good and upright relations between man and man, and between nation and nation, it is far from sufficient to rely on "innate" feelings of fairness and justice, as bitter experience has, unfortunately, often confirmed.

Ethics and integrity can only have a stable existence in the daily life of people and nations if these ethical and moral laws are supported by **super-human** foundations—that is, on the foundation of **kedushah** that is connected to the Creator of the world, Who is also the Creator of man.

For only the Creator Himself knows human nature thoroughly, with all its weaknesses. Therefore, only the Creator could create the true and lasting ethical and moral laws for humanity, for the individual as well as for the collective. These Divine laws are binding upon everyone without exception, everywhere, and at all times.

In light of the above, one can better appreciate the vital necessity of yeshivos in general, and Lubavitcher yeshivos in particular.

The time is past when it was thought that delving into Gemara, Mussar, and Chassidus was a necessity only for a Rav, a shochet, or the like, but that the average Jew, the "baal-habayis," did not need this. In the present time and in the current environment, it has become quite clear that delving into Torah and absorbing the atmosphere of *yiras-shamayim* (fear of Heaven) of the yeshiva is a vital necessity for **every** Jew, if one wishes to be a **complete** Jew—complete in **bein adam l’Makom** and in **bein adam l’chaveiro**—and to be able to fulfill to the fullest extent the *neshamah*-mission that Divine Providence has placed upon each and every one.

And this is precisely what distinguishes the Lubavitcher yeshivos, where the students, the "Tmimim," are educated toward **wholeness** in both aforementioned directions, where *ahavas-Hashem* (love of G-d) and *ahavas-HaTorah* (love of Torah) go hand-in-hand with *ahavas-Yisrael* (love of a fellow Jew) and come to their fullest expression in daily life.

I send my blessing and greetings to all the esteemed guests and participants in the annual celebration of the Merkaz Yeshivos Tomchei Tmimim Lubavitch, and I hope that each and every one will contribute their full share to the success of this endeavor, both in a material and in a spiritual sense.

With respect and blessing for great success in all the above.

## 8841

B"H, 18 Shevat, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To Rabbi Yehuda Samet, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings,

In response to your letter, in which you outline your work as a military chaplain and ask for guidance on how to succeed in your shlichus:

Based on the activities of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad, which has gained experience over many years through visits to military camps, meetings with students, and the like, the following points may be suggested:

1. Given the time constraints—as you write that you have only a few months remaining at the post—and the fact that most Jewish military personnel there remain for only six weeks, it is difficult to plan a program of work that progresses step-by-step.

Therefore, first and foremost, you must strive to awaken a general inspiration through words that come from the heart and enter the heart, in such a way that each individual asks himself what his role is as a Jew, what he must do to fulfill that role, and to what extent he is fulfilling his duty in this regard. The main thing is to arouse in him a desire to fulfill his role; for if the will is present, and he recognizes the greatness and pride of being a Jew, he will naturally strive to expand his knowledge even after he is no longer under your influence, and your influence will continue to resonate within him thereafter.

2. In order for this influence not to remain merely in the realm of speech, you must lead them to something tangible and practical—namely, a practical mitzvah every day. Beyond the inherent benefit of every practical action, there is the principle that "one mitzvah leads to another." Furthermore, the actual deed that the individual performs will constantly remind him of those heartfelt words that brought him to the action itself. Examples include putting on tefillin every weekday (at least for a few moments), reciting Shema, setting aside a coin for tzedakah every weekday morning, and the like.

3. Regarding those with families (doctors, etc.) who come there for a year or more, it is, of course, necessary to arrange a structured program for setting times for Torah study and for the observance of mitzvos, such as kashrus, tefillin for men, taharas hamishpachah, and so on—and in particular, the education of their children.

4. You must be mindful that among the military personnel, there are sometimes bochurim who come from observant backgrounds but think that in the army they are exempt from the yoke of mitzvos due to the difficulties involved, etc. It is well known that, on the contrary, the army provides an opportunity to observe mitzvos. Moreover, you must influence these individuals to recognize the responsibility and the privilege they have to influence others. Who knows if this is not their primary purpose for having been brought to that particular camp among new friends?

Since you write that you have only a few months left there, you will surely increase your efforts and courage in your influence, so that when your successor arrives, you will be able not only to hand him a practical work plan to continue, but also to point to the success and fruits of your influence.

If you require further assistance, printed materials, or the like, you may contact Tzeirei Agudas Chabad directly. Perhaps they can arrange a visit from one of their members to your camp if it would be of particular benefit.

With blessings for success and for good tidings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

***

8841

"One mitzvah leads to another": Avos 4:2.

## 8842

8842

B"H, Sunday, Parshas Mishpatim, 5725
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Brooklyn

To the Shomrei Shabbos Gemilas Chasadim Society
May Hashem protect them and grant them life,

Peace and Blessing!

I received with pleasure the news regarding your annual meeting for the benefit of the Gemach fund. May it be Hashem’s will that it be successful in every detail.

It is well known that in our holy Torah, even the order of the text is itself Torah and instruction. The section of laws (*Mishpatim*) is placed in proximity to the section regarding the Altar, to teach you that you must place the Sanhedrin near the Sanctuary.

This teaches us that even matters between man and his fellow—which constitute the vast majority of the laws in Parshas Mishpatim—must be in proximity to, and founded upon, the Sanctuary and holiness.

—This is also understood from the connection between the Ten Commandments, which begin with "I am Hashem your G-d" and conclude with "Do not covet"—

For only then is one’s conduct between man and his fellow truly complete.

And likewise, conversely: "That which is hateful to you, do not do to your fellow"—this is the entire Torah, and the rest is commentary; go and learn it.

May it be Hashem’s will that in your Gemach activities as well, it will be evident that they are founded upon the "I am Hashem your G-d" (in the singular) of every single Jew. For from this perspective, all are equal, one Father for all of us, and there is true love and brotherhood among them. All of this brings perfection and beauty to the Gemach and to the reconciliation that must accompany it.

The reward for all who engage in Gemach is explained and explicitly stated in many places in our Torah, the Torah of kindness, given by the merciful and gracious G-d who commanded us to walk in His ways. Indeed, Tzedakah is a literal Mitzvah of Hashem—it is exactly what the Holy One, Blessed be He, does Himself at all times.

With blessings for success in your holy work,

**M. Schneerson**

Enclosed is my contribution, *bli neder*, to the Gemach fund.
